Politico // Michael Grunwald \xe2\x80\x93 June 1, 2015*\n\n\n\nVietnam slaps tariffs of 70 percent on U.S. cars and machinery, 35 percent\non U.S. chemicals, 30 percent on U.S. biscuits and baked goods, and 25\npercent on U.S. recording equipment. Japan marks up our oranges 16 percent\nfrom June through November and 32 percent from December through May; it\nmarks up our beef exports 38.5 percent all year long. Cars made in America\nface a 30 percent tariff in Malaysia, which might not seem stiff compared\nto 50 percent on motorcycles or 35 percent on plywood, except that cars\nmade in Japan and other Asian nations don\xe2\x80\x99t face any tariff in Malaysia.\n\n\n\nThese burdensome overseas tariffs, provided to POLITICO by US Trade\nRepresentative Michael Froman, are the kind of problems President Obama\nhopes to address with the free trade deal known as the Trans-Pacific\nPartnership, which has not yet been finalized but has recently erupted into\none of the most contentious topics on Washington\xe2\x80\x99s agenda.\n\n\n\nThe public debate has focused on the adequacy of TPP\xe2\x80\x99s environmental and\nlabor safeguards, its potential to feather the nests of well-connected\npharmaceutical, software and finance interests, and the secrecy of its\nnegotiations. But the heart of the deal is an effort by the twelve\nparticipating countries to phase out tariffs and other export barriers for\nmore than 11,000 categories of commodities, and Froman is frustrated that\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t getting more attention. In an interview with POLITICO, he said\nexport-supported U.S. jobs pay 13 to 18 percent more than the average job,\nand argued that freer trade along the Pacific Rim would create a lot more\nof them.\n\n\n\nSo I asked if Froman would give me a rundown of actual tariffs he\xe2\x80\x99s trying\nto remove, and he agreed. Global tariffs are not secret information\xe2\x80\x94they\xe2\x80\x99re\nkept on file at the World Customs Organization, and Froman has mentioned a\nfew in speeches\xe2\x80\x94but they\xe2\x80\x99re rarely seen in large quantities outside\ntechnical reports, and Froman hopes that taking a bunch of examples public\nwill help build support for TPP. The deal is not done yet, so Froman can\xe2\x80\x99t\ndiscuss how much the various import restrictions he cited will be reduced,\nbut the point of the negotiations is to reduce them. There are plenty of\nlegitimate concerns about free trade and TPP that have nothing to do with\ntariffs, some of them highlighted in a critique in POLITICO last week  by a\nDemocrat who read a draft, but Froman believes most Americans would at\nleast back the administration\xe2\x80\x99s efforts to tear down these tariffs.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s been a lot easier for our critics to focus on this controversial\nelement or that controversial element, rather than what we\xe2\x80\x99re trying to\nachieve,\xe2\x80\x9d Froman told me.\n\n\n\nFroman and I spoke last Friday in his office a block from the White House;\nI noticed the 1934 law repealing the notorious Smoot-Hawley tariffs, which\nhelped drag America into the Great Depression, hanging on the wall of his\nanteroom. Critics like New York Times columnist Paul Krugman  and MIT\neconomist Simon Johnson have suggested that TPP isn\xe2\x80\x99t really about breaking\ndown Smoot-Hawley-style barriers, because previous trade deals over the\nlast three decades have already created a low-tariff world. But while\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s mostly true of the U.S., which imposes no duties on 70 percent of\nour imports\xe2\x80\x9480 percent from TPP nations\xe2\x80\x94Froman offered a slew of data and\nexamples suggesting that\xe2\x80\x99s not always true abroad.\n\n\n\nOverall, the U.S. imposes an average tariff of 1.4 percent on foreign\ngoods, less than half the average for the rest of the nations Froman is\nnegotiating with, barely a fourth the average in Vietnam and Malaysia. And\nit can get much worse for specific industries and products. TPP nations\nhave tariffs ranging up to 100 percent on textiles, 87 percent on corn, and\n75 percent on consumer goods, not to mention selected Japanese tariffs that\namount to 189 percent on U.S. shoes and a don\xe2\x80\x99t-even-think-about-it 778\npercent on U.S. rice above a certain annual quota.\n\n\n\nEven our friendly trading partner to the north has some brutal\nanti-American protectionism on its books. The North American Free Trade\nAgreement of 1994 broke down a slew of barriers between the U.S. and\nCanada, but it exempted the poultry and dairy industries, which is why U.S.\neggs face tariffs of up to 163.5 percent\xe2\x80\x94and not less than 79.9 cents per\ndozen\xe2\x80\x94in the land of ice hockey and eh. U.S. yogurt, milk, cheese, and\nfrozen chicken all face tariffs between 237.5 percent and 249 percent in\nCanada.\n\n\n\nIn some TPP countries, tariffs not only make U.S. exports more expensive,\nthey make U.S. exports more expensive relative to goods from other\ncountries. For example, America\xe2\x80\x99s construction equipment only faces a 5\npercent tariff in New Zealand, but construction equipment from Southeast\nAsia faces no tariffs there. Vietnam slaps tariffs of 20 percent on beauty\nand skin products from the U.S. while importing similar products from its\nAsian neighbors duty-free. Vietnam\xe2\x80\x99s tariffs for U.S. poultry are also\ntwice as high as for Australian poultry, and its tariffs for switches,\nrelays and fuses made in America are four times as high as for the same\nproducts made in China. Our polymers and tree nuts face similar\ndiscrimination in Malaysia, as do our aluminum bars and frozen potatoes in\nJapan. American vineyards are among the biggest losers from Japanese\nprotectionism, facing a 50 percent wine tariff while their Chilean and\nAustralian rivals face none.\n\n\n\nFroman provided a wide-ranging list of categories where TPP countries still\nmaintain tariffs, from health products to communications technologies to\nhigh-tech instruments to fish. The biggest category was agriculture, where\nU.S. farmers and ranchers face tariffs on apples, cotton, wheat, soybeans,\nfresh and processed vegetables, pork, feeds, and just about everything\nelse. Overall, American exporters shipped more than $700 billion worth of\nstuff to TPP nations last year, so better access to TPP markets could bring\na lot of additional revenue into the United States.\n\n\n\nWhile global tariffs clearly have not disappeared, it\xe2\x80\x99s true that they\xe2\x80\x99re\ngenerally much lower than they were decades ago, even where they\xe2\x80\x99re much\nhigher than ours. The U.S. already has bilateral trade deals with many TPP\nnations. But Froman says the TPP will also tear down the kind of\n\xe2\x80\x9cnon-tariff barriers\xe2\x80\x9d that countries have used to protect their industries\nwithout violating the earlier deals, like sudden declarations that U.S.\nturkey products are unsafe for Australian consumers, or selective laws\nrestricting U.S. express delivery services from doing business in Malaysia.\nThe TPP is also expected to prohibit laws requiring foreign businesses to\nlocate their computer servers in the country, as well as excessively\nburdensome regulations on global e-commerce. These kinds of barriers can be\nparticularly troublesome in finance, data processing, engineering and other\nservice industries where U.S. firms tend to be prominent.\n\n\n\nIt must be said that removing some non-tariff barriers\xe2\x80\x94for example, forcing\nTPP nations to respect patents for expensive U.S. pharmaceuticals, or\ncopyrights for U.S. software\xe2\x80\x94would not necessarily be good for the world\xe2\x80\x99s\nconsumers. But they would be good for the U.S. industries in question. It\nshould also be mentioned that some U.S. industries\xe2\x80\x94notably textiles,\nfootwear, sugar, and dairy\xe2\x80\x94still enjoy trade protections that would be\nreduced through TPP. But Froman said the administration is working hard to\nmake sure their concerns are addressed, and the textile industry actually\ncame out in favor of the Trade Promotion Authority \xe2\x80\x9cfast-track\xe2\x80\x9d bill that\nis considered a prerequisite for TPP\xe2\x80\x99s passage.\n\n\n\nFroman made TPP sound so rosy for U.S. companies that I finally asked him:\nWhat\xe2\x80\x99s in it for the other countries? The answer, he said, is a combination\nof improved access to U.S. markets, greatly improved access to Japan, the\nopportunity to lure U.S. businesses away from a restrictive China, and a\nkind of Good Housekeeping seal of approval as a member of the free-trade\nclub. I also suggested to Froman that if TPP will really do so much more to\nlower trade barriers abroad, it must seem weird to his negotiating partners\nthat the deal is so hotly disputed in the U.S., especially among members of\nObama\xe2\x80\x99s party. He said the fraught domestic debate actually bolsters the\nU.S. negotiating position, because those partners are savvy enough to\nunderstand that a deal without strong support from the U.S. agriculture and\nbusiness communities and strong labor and environmental protections would\nhave no chance of passing.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey follow our politics. They read POLITICO Pro,\xe2\x80\x9d Froman said. \xe2\x80\x9cThey know\nwe\xe2\x80\x99re not bluffing, and that has helped us strengthen this deal.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nFroman refused to discuss what the final TPP will do to any of the specific\ntariffs and general tariff categories we discussed, but he hinted that just\nabout all of them will be cut or eliminated. In any case, when the deal is\ndone, the text will be published at ustr.gov\xe2\x80\x94and POLITICO will do a\nfollow-up comparing the before and after tariffs on U.S. products.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot wait,\xe2\x80\x9d Froman said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s going to clear up a lot of\nmisconceptions.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n*OPINIONS/EDITORIALS/BLOGS*\n\n\n\n*The more the merrier: Democratic candidates for 2016\n<http://www.cnn.com/2015/06/01/opinions/pfeiffer-democrats-more-candidates-the-better/index.html>\n// CNN // Dan Pfeiffer \xe2\x80\x93 June 1, 2015*\n\n\n\n(CNN)This is something I don\'t find myself saying very often: I agree with\nKarl Rove.\n\n\n\nIn a recent post on Medium, the former strategist for President George W.\nBush made the case that the historically large size of the GOP primary\nwasn\'t a disadvantage, rebutting some conventional wisdom that a\n20-candidate field would be a net negative for the Grand Ole Party. There\nare so many candidates running that some have compared it to a clown car\nand not just because Donald Trump is thinking of running.\n\n\n\nWhile the Democratic primary doesn\'t resemble the clown car nature of the\nRepublican field (in part because it\'s not filled with clowns), it did get\na little bigger this weekend, with former Maryland Gov. Martin O\'Malley\njoining Hillary Clinton and Sen. Bernie Sanders of Vermont in the race. And\nwhat Rove wrote about the Republicans is even more true of the Democrats. A\ncompetitive primary is not just not a disadvantage as Rove writes; it will\nactually be an advantage. A fight for the Democratic nomination is good for\nthe party and will help us, not hurt us, next year in November.\n\n\n\nFirst, competitive primaries make better general election candidates. As\ndivisive, heated and long as the 2008 Democratic primary was, there is no\nquestion that Barack Obama was a much stronger nominee because of Clinton.\nHe honed his skills as a candidate, sharpened his message and became a\nstronger debater. He learned how to take a punch and throw one right back.\nMost importantly, he faced political adversity time and again and learned\nhow to handle it.\n\n\n\nIf Clinton is our nominee, which is at this point the overwhelmingly most\nlikely scenario, she will be better prepared to take on Jeb Bush, Marco\nRubio or whoever else the Republicans nominate because Sanders and O\'Malley\nmade her that way.\n\n\n\nSecond, competitive primaries make better general election campaigns. In\n2008, Obama won Indiana, North Carolina and Virginia in large part because\nwe ran hard in those states in the primaries. The primary gave us the\nopportunity to build large grass-roots organizations with well-trained\norganizers and volunteers.\n\n\n\nWe were able to try out our get out the vote machinery in almost every\nstate in the country months before the election. Iowa and New Hampshire may\ndecide the presidency in 2016, and the Democratic nominee will be better\npositioned to win those states because of the organizations built and\ntested in the primaries.\n\n\n\nFinally, competitive primaries make better political parties. Many of the\nissues that have driven the Democratic Party in recent decades -- health\ncare, the Bush tax cuts, the Bush wars -- have been largely resolved during\nthe Obama presidency. The party is grappling a bit with what comes next --\nwith which issues and solutions are going to animate us as Democrats.\n\n\n\nUnlike the Republicans, Democrats are not a collection of competing and\noften contradictory interests. We are unified, we know what we stand for\nand are confident in our ideals, but how do we apply those ideals to a new\nset of evolving challenges?\n\n\n\nHistorically, these questions have been answered in robust primary debates.\nIf O\'Malley and Sanders run good, idea-driven campaigns, there will be a\nrobust debate about the future of the Democratic Party that will make us\nbetter in the 2016 general election and in years to come.\n\n\n\nIt\'s clear that the Clinton campaign understands this. They are running\nvery hard and building the sorts of campaigns in Iowa and New Hampshire\nthat are designed to win tough races. They are running like an underdog,\nnot the biggest favorite in history.\n\n\n\nThere is an important caveat -- one that Rove hints at in his column about\nthe Republican side: Competitive, hard-fought primaries are good until they\naren\'t. There is a moment where they can go from competitive to irreparably\ndivisive. The Obama-Clinton race came close to crossing that line a couple\nof times, but never did because both candidates worked hard to repair the\nbreach.\n\n\n\nThere should be robust debate about ideas and issues, about who would make\na better president and leader of the party. But when Democrats use\nRepublican talking points to describe other Democrats, that crosses a line.\nIf that happens, it will be incumbent on the whole party -- from President\nObama on down -- to call foul. Fortunately, the risk of this primary\ngetting out of hand is very small, given the individuals involved and the\nshared sense that winning this election is critical for the future of the\nissues that matter to Democrats.\n\n\n\nReporters, pundits and people who own television stations in places such as\nIowa are rooting for a fight for the Democratic nomination, and Democrats\nshould be, too. A competition, not a coronation, is what\'s best for our\nchances in 2016.\n\n\n\n*Why Hillary should stop running as the incumbent\n<http://www.washingtonpost.com/blogs/achenblog/wp/2015/06/01/why-hillary-should-stop-running-as-the-incumbent/>\n// WaPo // Joel Achenbach \xe2\x80\x93 June 1, 2015*\n\n\n\nJack Shafer nailed it when he wrote recently in Politico that Hillary\nClinton is not running for president, but running as president. She rarely\ntakes a question from a reporter. News organizations spend a small fortune\ndispatching  reporters and photographers and camera crews to follow her\naround, but they\xe2\x80\x99re treated like carry-on baggage.\n\n\n\nOne can understand why anyone in the Clinton camp would have a jaundiced\nview of the news media and might view news in general as something that\nshould be planned and crafted in advance, protected against unsupervised\nnuances and unhelpful twists, and approved by at least four layers of\nmanagement, including the candidate herself, Bill, the folks at the\nFoundation and their various foreign benefactors. I\xe2\x80\x99m sympathetic, in other\nwords. Still, the obvious downside is that it makes the candidacy\nuninteresting. As a citizen, you have to force yourself to pay attention.\nThe Democrats don\xe2\x80\x99t have much of a contest, with all due respect to\nfiring-on-both-cylinders candidates Bernie Sanders and Martin O\xe2\x80\x99Malley.\n\n\n\nSuccessful candidacies and successful presidencies have a point to them;\ninevitability isn\xe2\x80\x99t enough. Here\xe2\x80\x99s a question that I throw out in all\nsincerity: Why is Clinton running? No doubt she has explained her candidacy\nmany times and in many ways, and I just missed those news reports (and I am\nnot being sarcastic here, it\xe2\x80\x99s just, you know, I\xe2\x80\x99ve been distracted by\nother stuff, like the destiny of the species and the fate of the universe\nand whatnot). She certainly has an impressive r\xc3\xa9sum\xc3\xa9 and most folks who are\nnot hard-core haters would view her as qualified to be president. But\nwhat\xe2\x80\x99s her candidacy about?\n\n\n\nThose of us who have been around awhile remember the telling moment when\nRoger Mudd asked Teddy Kennedy why he wanted to be president. The senator\nfumbled the answer. The real answer was: Because he could. Because he might\nwin. Because he was a Kennedy.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHillary: It\xe2\x80\x99s My Turn\xe2\x80\x9d is Clinton\xe2\x80\x99s campaign motto, my friend and\ncolleague Marc Fisher joked the other day at a panel discussion at our alma\nmater.\n\n\n\nMeanwhile, on the Republican side, there are 137 people running for\npresident (including Lindsey Graham, who is announcing today). Do we have\nto pay attention to all these folks? No, not all of them. Not Donald Trump,\nfor example. But the standard that should guide us is not how well a\ncandidate is doing in the polls. Richard Just, another of the panelists,\nand the editor of National Journal, made a great point about news coverage\nof presidential candidacies: What they say matters, even if they have close\nto zero, or literally zero, chance of winning.\n\n\n\nThis is how political parties are shaped, Richard said. This is how a party\ndefines itself and how ideas get into circulation even if they will take\nmany years to gain traction. Goldwater was trounced in 1964, but his\nconservative ideals found their way to the White House when his acolyte\nRonald Reagan won in 1980.\n\n\n\nDana Milbank correctly notes that many of Sen. Rand Paul\xe2\x80\x99s statements in\nrecent months sound more like Democratic positions than Republican\npositions, and this will make it hard for Paul to capture his party\xe2\x80\x99s\nnomination. But I suspect he is playing a longer game. His people told me\n(when I did my profile on the senator) that they don\xe2\x80\x99t think the GOP can\nwin a national presidential election unless they expand the coalition,\nbringing in more young people, minorities and people who are normally\ndisaffected and don\xe2\x80\x99t go to the polls. The Republican candidate has won a\npopular-vote majority only once in the past six elections. That\xe2\x80\x99s likely a\nmulti-election project. And it\xe2\x80\x99s why Paul is an interesting candidate \xe2\x80\x94\neven if he\xe2\x80\x99s not likely to be the Republican nominee in 2016.\n\n\n\n*Populism Strains Both Parties; Can They Adapt?\n<http://www.wsj.com/articles/populist-election-themes-show-strains-in-both-parties-1433177188>\n// WSJ // Gerald F. Seib \xe2\x80\x93 June 1, 2015*\n\n\n\nIs a third political party straining to break out in America?\n\n\n\nListen to the early sounds of the 2016 presidential campaign, in which\ncandidates of the left and the right sound almost identical populist,\nanti-Wall Street, antiestablishment themes, and the idea doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem so\ncrazy.\n\n\n\nWhen you have Republican presidential contenders opposing free-trade\nagreements, and at least one backing an increase in the minimum wage; when\nDemocratic firebrand Bernie Sanders (technically an independent to begin\nwith) is speaking to overflow crowds; and when left and right come together\nto halt a prominent national-security program backed by the foreign-policy\nestablishment\xe2\x80\x94well, something is going on.\n\n\n\nLet\xe2\x80\x99s stipulate up front that a serious third party isn\xe2\x80\x99t likely to emerge,\nat least not in this cycle. A viable third party requires money and an\noutsize personality (think Ross Perot), and it isn\xe2\x80\x99t clear those elements\nexist right now.\n\n\n\nStill, it\xe2\x80\x99s possible to see the ideological foundations for one. Similar\npopulist refrains are coming from a diverse cast of political characters\nwith real followings: Mr. Sanders and Sen. Elizabeth Warren on the left,\nand Rick Santorum, Mike Huckabee and Sen. Rand Paul on the right. Listen to\nthe remarks of declared 2016 contenders and you can almost hear a potential\nthird-party platform emerging. Here would be its main elements:\n\n\n\nBig banks and Wall Street have too much power in Washington. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have come\nto take our country back from the special interests that use Washington as\ntheir personal piggy bank,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Paul said in opening his presidential\ncampaign. Said Mr. Sanders: \xe2\x80\x9cWhen we talk about power, we talk about Wall\nStreet. In my view, it is time to break up the largest financial\ninstitutions in this country.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nThe economic system is stacked against the middle class, and government\npolicies are making that worse. In his announcement, Mr. Santorum referred\nto \xe2\x80\x9cworkers\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cworking families\xe2\x80\x9d a dozen times, and said they have\nsuffered because of the policies of \xe2\x80\x9cbig government and big business.\xe2\x80\x9d He\nhas broken with GOP orthodoxy by supporting a \xe2\x80\x9cmodest\xe2\x80\x9d minimum-wage\nincrease and continuation of the Export-Import Bank to bolster\nmanufacturing.\n\n\n\nFree-trade deals are bad for America and U.S. workers. Speaking from the\nright, Mr. Huckabee declared: \xe2\x80\x9cWe don\xe2\x80\x99t create good jobs for Americans by\nentering into unbalanced trade deals that forgo congressional scrutiny, and\nthen looking the other way as the law is ignored so that we can import\nlow-wage labor, undercut American workers, and drive wages lower than the\nDead Sea.\xe2\x80\x9d From the left, Mr. Sanders declared: \xe2\x80\x9cThe bottom line of these\ntrade agreements is to force American workers to compete against people in\ncountries around the world who make pennies on the hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nBig money is corrupting politics. \xe2\x80\x9cWorking families don\xe2\x80\x99t need another\npresident tied to big government or big money,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Santorum said in\nannouncing his candidacy. Said Mr. Huckabeein announcing his: \xe2\x80\x9cI never have\nbeen and I\xe2\x80\x99m not going to be the favored candidate of those in the\nWashington-to-Wall Street corridor of power.\xe2\x80\x9d He added that his campaign\nwill be \xe2\x80\x9cfunded and fueled not by billionaires but by working people.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nThe U.S. is overextended abroad. \xe2\x80\x9cI am vigorously opposed to an endless,\nperpetual war in the Middle East,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Sanders. Said Mr. Paul: \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s\nquit building bridges in foreign countries and use that money to build some\nbridges here at home.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nBig brother is dangerous. The effort to stop the National Security Agency\xe2\x80\x99s\ncollection of cellphone bulk data has, of course, become a signature issue\nof Mr. Paul, who has tied the Senate in knots over the issue and finds\nhimself aligned with many liberals as a result. But he isn\xe2\x80\x99t alone; Mr.\nHuckabee said on Fox News over the weekend that U.S. officials should get a\nwarrant before collecting phone data: \xe2\x80\x9cIf this is so effective, how come it\nhasn\xe2\x80\x99t resulted in the foiled terrorist plots?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nObviously, there are many core issues on which the antiestablishment forces\nof left and right disagree, climate change and the virtues of Obamacare\nprominent among them. Still, it\xe2\x80\x99s worth noting that Mr. Sanders packed a\nhall with 3,000 enthusiastic supporters over the weekend in Minneapolis,\nwith more waiting outside because there wasn\xe2\x80\x99t room for them. Meantime, Mr.\nSantorum\xe2\x80\x99s overtures to the working class help show that the Republican\nParty now includes a sizable blue-collar, Wal-Mart wing.\n\n\n\nAt a minimum, mainstream candidates in both parties will have to hew to\nsome populist themes emerging from the antiestablishment crowd. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are\nvotes in both primaries to be found for the candidates that say, \xe2\x80\x98We want\nto change the system and level the playing field,\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9d says Democratic\npollster Peter Hart, who helps conduct The Wall Street Journal/NBC News\npoll.\n\n\n\n*How to Kill the Background Briefing\n<http://www.nationaljournal.com/politics/how-to-kill-the-background-briefing-20150601>\n// National Journal // Ron Fournier \xe2\x80\x93 June 1, 2015*\n\n\n\nI\'ve got a plan to bust the background briefing racket. Of the many ways\nthat modern journalists cede power to authority, none is easier to fix than\nthe notion that government officials are allowed to gather several\nreporters in a room or on a conference call, spew their clever lines of\nlies and spin, and declare it all "on background"\xe2\x80\x94shielded from\naccountability "on condition of anonymity."\n\n\n\nReporters are increasingly allowing public relations teams to set the terms\nfor coverage of politics, business, sports, and even entertainment. It\'s a\ntroubling trend for an industry in flux. But the media in Washington are\nthe most unnecessarily and dangerously cowed.\n\n\n\nWhen reporters call the shots, anonymous sources are vital to uncovering\ngovernment secrets and wrongdoing (Bob Woodward and Carl Bernstein used\nMark Felt and other whistleblowers to chase Richard Nixon out of office).\nWhen government officials call the shots, there is no incentive to tell the\ntruth, no punishment for deception, and the journalism itself is diminished\nby canned news.\n\n\n\nIn rare cases, approved in advance by journalists, an anonymous source\ntalking to a bunch of reporters might serve a public service. For instance,\nsecurity details for a presidential trip to a war zone may need to be\nshared privately with the traveling media corps. Most editors would\nconsider that a good case for a briefing that shields the identity of the\ngovernment officials and embargoes news of the trip itself.\n\n\n\nBut in recent years, public relations officials have been allowed to assume\nthat they get to decide the terms for briefing reporters, and what was one\na unique courtesy is now a daily disservice to the public. Just last week,\nthe Obama administration sought to to build public support for extending\npost-911 domestic spying programs it lied about until Edward Snowden blew\nthe whistle.\n\n\n\nThe New York Times reported:\n\n\n\n"What you\'re doing, essentially, is you\'re playing national security\nRussian roulette," one senior administration official said of allowing the\npowers to lapse. That prospect appears increasingly likely with the\nmeasure, the USA Freedom Act, stalled and lawmakers in their home states\nand districts during a congressional recess.\n\n\n\n"We\'re in uncharted waters," another senior member of the administration\nsaid at a briefing organized by the White House, where three officials\nspoke with reporters about the consequences of inaction by Congress. "We\nhave not had to confront addressing the terrorist threat without these\nauthorities, and it\'s going to be fraught with unnecessary risk."\n\n\n\nNow ask yourself: If these scare tactics are legitimate, why wouldn\'t\nadministration officials allow their names to be public? (Josh Earnest had\nthe chance today, and wouldn\'t.) When they refused, why would the Times and\nseveral other news organizations publish the quotes?\n\n\n\nThe standard rule for using anonymous sources, published in Associated\nPress stylebooks used in almost every newsroom, is: "Whenever possible, we\npursue information on the record. When the source insists on background or\noff-the-record ground rules, we must adhere to a strict set of guidelines."\n\n\n\nFirst, the material is information "and not opinion or speculation, and is\nvital to the news report." Second, the information is not available on the\nrecord. Third, the source is reliable.\n\n\n\nThe White House briefing was a total rule-breaker: 1) The material was\nopinion and speculation; 2) There is no shortage of on-the-record hyperbole\nabout the National Security Agency program; 3) The Obama administration,\nlike the Bush White House, routinely deceives the public about NSA\'s\nactivities.\n\n\n\nThese are not credible sources.\n\n\n\nJust last week, reporters wrote and broadcast material from a background\nbriefing conducted by Hillary Clinton advisers who insisted they not be\nidentified. This from Politico:\n\n\n\nAt Clinton campaign headquarters, meanwhile, senior campaign officials told\nreporters Thursday at a background briefing that Hillary Clinton has not\nbeen damaged or distracted by the revelations about her family\'s foundation.\n\n\n\nAgain: If that paragraph is true (it\'s not), why wouldn\'t the Clinton\nadvisers put their names behind it? Why did the media report anonymous,\ninaccurate spin? Google the Bush White House. Watch how the GOP\npresidential campaigns deal with the media. The background briefing is a\nbipartisan racket. So why do journalists let their sources get away with it?\n\n\n\nFear: Reporters and editors don\'t want to get beat by competitors who bow\nto the terms of a public relations team. They don\'t stop to realize very\nlittle news comes from these briefings, and none of it is worth ceding\nprecious leverage to the government.\n\n\n\nHabit: It\'s a lazy way out. I know, because it\'s a route I\'ve taken. As a\nreporter and editor, I\'ve also taken part in countless (and useless)\nprotests against background briefings.\n\n\n\nVanity: There actually are some reporters who prefer anonymous sources over\nnamed ones, because they believe "on condition of anonymity" creates the\nperception that they\'re plugged into their beats. They\'re fooling\nthemselves and, worse, their audiences.\n\n\n\nAccess: Reporters are increasingly kept apart from the people they cover.\nThey don\'t recognize that the way to get access is by raising hell\xe2\x80\x94by\nmaking yourself such a source of surprise and disruption that the people\nyou cover realize they need to get to know you.\n\n\n\nJonathan Allen of Vox, one of the better reporters on the beat, explained\nwhy he accepted the Clinton ground rules last week. "These officials are\npeople who could easily avoid interacting with reporters for the entire\ncampaign if they chose," he wrote. "Given the option of talking to them or\nnot, major news organizations chose the former."\n\n\n\nI get it. I used to think that way. But I\'ve learned over the years that I\ndon\'t need to talk to campaign people. They need to talk to me. Freeze me\nout? Fine\xe2\x80\x94that\'s just more time to talk to rival campaigns, political\nscientists, and the best sources on any campaign: voters.\n\n\n\nEven the dimmest public relations officials eventually learn that they\ncan\'t put a good reporter on ice and, even if they could, they would be\nfreezing themselves out of the reporter\'s stories.\n\n\n\nSecond, why would I let the Clinton campaign\xe2\x80\x94or any entity I cover\xe2\x80\x94give me\n"the option" to choose? On this and so many other ways the media has ceded\npower, I say flip the script. Make them choose. Put them on the sorry end\nof a devil\'s choice.\n\n\n\nWhich gets me to my plan to bust the briefing racket.\n\n\n\nIf you\'re a reporter who gets sucked into a "background briefing," don\'t\nlet anonymous spin creep into your stories. Ignore it (National Journal\nattended the Clinton briefing but didn\'t write it up). Or call it out, as\nJohn Harwood did here: "How\'s Hillary Doing? Wish We Could Tell You"\n\n\n\nWish I could tell you more. But they said very little.\n\n\n\nNotice that I typed very little and not "very little," because under the\nground rules of Thursday\'s briefing reporters were not allowed to quote\ntheir words directly.\n\n\n\nYou\'re not missing much.\n\n\n\nFor all its understated brilliance, Harwood\'s column won\'t put a dent in\nthe background briefing racket. It was, after all, a retroactive hit\ndirected at just one PR team on behalf of just one reporter.\n\n\n\nThe media should be more proactive, like Adam Nagourney of The New York\nTimes, who tweeted today the details of Clinton\'s presidential announcement\nevent and directly attributed them to spokesman Jesse Ferguson. The aide\nhad emailed the information to reporters, presuming that he could deem the\nmaterial "on background."\n\n\n\nWrong.\n\n\n\nIf you\'re a reporter, go to your email now and write to the director of\ncommunications for whatever beat you cover \xe2\x80\x93 a campaign, an agency, a\nbusiness, a sports team, whatever.\n\n\n\n"Dear ___," your note starts.\n\n\n\n"I just wanted to remind you of a long tradition in journalism: Everything\na reporter sees and hears is on the record and attributable directly to the\nsource unless the reporter and source mutually agree in advance to other\nterms."\n\n\n\n"That means, of course, that every briefing I attend, in person or on\nconference call, will be on the record unless you get my permission in\nadvance. (Knowing my editor\'s commitment to transparency and\naccountability, I suspect permission will only be granted in extreme\ncases.)"\n\n\n\nCongratulations. You just forced the PR types to choose: Do we want a\nbriefing bad enough to play by this person\'s rules? The answer will almost\nalways be yes, and when it\'s not, you won\'t miss much. Hell, you\'re a good\nenough reporter to find out what\'s said at briefings you don\'t attend.\n\n\n\nYou might want to add to your note:\n\n\n\n"Same applies to every email you send me. Writing "OFF THE RECORD" at the\ntop doesn\'t make it so.\n\n\n\n"I\'m always open to using anonymous sources, but only when we\'re dealing\nwith important information (not spin) and when my editor and I determine\nit\'s in the best interest of our audience."\n\n\n\nIf you\'re an editor, consider giving your reporters cover (these are your\nrules, after all). Email the communications director at every beat your\nnewsroom covers, and tell them:\n\n\n\n"If the briefing material is important and true, there\'s no good reason to\nhide the source\'s identity. Unless you get prior permission from one of my\nreporters, all briefings are on the record. Same for your emails.\n\n\n\nRemind them who\'s in charge.\n\n\n\n* NYT The Upshot: G.O.P. Women in Congress: Why So Few?\n<http://www.nytimes.com/2015/06/02/upshot/gop-women-in-congress-why-so-few.html?rref=upshot&abt=0002&abg=0>\n// NYT // Derek Willis \xe2\x80\x93 June 1, 2015*\n\n\n\nThe rising number of women in Congress can obscure another trend: The\nnumber of Republican women has remained roughly stagnant for more than a\ndecade.\n\n\n\nAlthough women in both parties have increased their numbers in Congress\nduring the past 25 years, the share of Democratic women \xe2\x80\x94 now nearly 33\npercent \xe2\x80\x94 has continued to climb, while the Republican female share has\nleveled off since hitting 10 percent during the mid-2000s. And political\npolarization seems to be a major reason.\n\n\n\nModerate Republican women \xe2\x80\x94 think of Olympia Snowe, the former Maine\nsenator, or Connie Morella, the former Maryland congresswoman \xe2\x80\x94 were once\ncommon in the party, according to research by Danielle Thomsen, a political\nscientist at Duke. But moderate Republicans of both genders are nearly gone\nfrom Congress today. Some conservative women, like Senator Joni Ernst of\nIowa, have been elected, but there are relatively few of them in a\ntraditional pipeline to Congress: state legislatures. In other words, the\ngap is likely to persist for some time.\n\n\n\nAs one measure of the gap, 17 Republican women have served in the\n\nSenate in its history, and 14 Democratic women currently serve in the\n\nSenate.\n\n\n\nThe pattern is part of a larger gender disparity in American politics, of\ncourse. In 2012, 55 percent of women voted for President Obama, while 52\npercent of men voted for Mitt Romney, according to exit polls. The gap\nfigures to continue, if not widen, in 2016, with Democrats seeming likely\nto nominate a woman for president and Republicans likely to nominate a man.\n\n\n\nThere is plenty of research that the presence of women in legislative\nbodies makes a difference, particularly on the policies that many female\nlawmakers prioritize, such as health care and children\xe2\x80\x99s issues. Interviews\nwith women in Congress by the Center for American Women and Politics have\nfound that many see themselves as \xe2\x80\x9csurrogate representatives\xe2\x80\x9d for women in\ngeneral.\n\n\n\nA root cause of the gap is that Democratic women who are potential\ncongressional candidates tend to fit comfortably with the liberal ideology\nof their party\xe2\x80\x99s primary voters, while many potential female Republican\ncandidates do not adhere to the conservative ideology of their primary\nvoters.\n\n\n\nBut it\xe2\x80\x99s not just moderate Republican women who have been affected by the\npolarization of the parties. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you look at the moderate men, they\xe2\x80\x99re not\nthere, either,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, and conservative Democrats in state legislatures\nalso aren\xe2\x80\x99t running for Congress.\n\n\n\nMs. Thomsen found that one in five Republican state legislators of either\nsex could be described as moderate, based on their voting history and\ndonors, but moderates were not nearly as eager as conservatives to run for\nCongress.\n\n\n\nIsn\'t the real reason there are less females in Congress the fact that less\nof them aspire to go into politics?\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cConservative Republican men and women state legislators are equally likely\nto run for Congress, but women are outnumbered five to one,\xe2\x80\x9d Ms. Thomsen\nsaid.\n\n\n\nSeparate research, by Shauna Shames, a political scientist at Rutgers\nUniversity, suggests that women with fiscally conservative and socially\nliberal views particularly struggle to find a party home. \xe2\x80\x9cA whole lot of\nwomen who could run are not running, because they know they will not be\nsupported and don\xe2\x80\x99t feel that they fit in the party anymore,\xe2\x80\x9d Ms. Shames\nsaid.\n\n\n\nThe Republican women who have run in congressional primaries over the past\n25 years have been as conservative as Republican men, according to a study\nproduced this year by Political Parity, a program that pushes for more\nwomen in Congress. (Ms. Thomsen and Ms. Shames both worked on the study).\nThere simply have not been very many highly conservative female candidates,\ncompared with men.\n\n\n\nOn the Democratic side, the situation is different. Female candidates for\nCongress were more liberal on average than their male counterparts, the\nstudy found, helping them do well in party primaries, which emphasize\nideological purity.\n\n\n\nMs. Thomsen\xe2\x80\x99s research does suggest that the number of conservative women\nin Congress will increase in coming years, because the number of\nconservative women in state legislatures has recently risen, albeit slowly.\nThe three most recently elected female Republican senators \xe2\x80\x94\n\nMs. Ernst, Shelley Moore Capito of West Virginia and Deb Fischer of\n\nNebraska \xe2\x80\x94 are all conservative and all served in their state legislatures.\n\n\n\nYet the share of congressional Democrats who are female may also continue\nrising, which could keep the current gap from shrinking.\n\n\n\n*A Bad Voting Ban in Maryland\n<http://www.nytimes.com/2015/06/01/opinion/a-bad-voting-ban-in-maryland.html?ref=politics>\n// NYT // The Editorial Board \xe2\x80\x93 June 1, 2015*\n\n\n\nWhat is the logic behind state laws that deny the vote to people who have\nbeen convicted of a felony, even after they are released from prison? The\nshort and easy answer is: there isn\xe2\x80\x99t any.\n\n\n\nFor a longer, nonsensical answer, ask Gov. Larry Hogan of Maryland, who on\nMay 22 thwarted strong majorities in both houses of the state legislature\nto veto a bill that would have restored voting rights to about 40,000\nMaryland residents currently on probation or parole.\n\n\n\nSome of these people never went to prison at all. Those who did are now in\nthe process of reintegrating themselves into their communities. Restoring\ntheir ability to have a say in choosing their elected representatives \xe2\x80\x94 the\nmost fundamental of all democratic rights \xe2\x80\x94 should be an obvious element of\nthat reintegration. But to Mr. Hogan, a Republican, people on probation or\nparole are \xe2\x80\x9cstill serving their time as a debt to society for their\nactions,\xe2\x80\x9d and so it is appropriate to continue to bar them from the ballot\nbox.\n\n\n\nThis is terrible reasoning. Voting bans \xe2\x80\x9cdefy the principles \xe2\x80\x94 of\naccountability and rehabilitation \xe2\x80\x94 that guide our criminal justice\npolicies,\xe2\x80\x9d as former Attorney General Eric Holder said last year in calling\nfor their repeal.\n\n\n\nThere has never been a whit of evidence that restoring voting rights has\nany negative impact on society, and yet disenfranchisement laws have a long\nand often racist pedigree in American history. States passed them in the\n19th century to block African-Americans from voting. Today about six\nmillion people nationwide are disenfranchised because of a criminal record,\nwith blacks disproportionately burdened. In Maryland, nearly two-thirds of\npeople who can\xe2\x80\x99t vote because of a felony conviction are African-American.\n\n\n\nThe governor\xe2\x80\x99s veto was all the more vexing given what has been happening\nin Baltimore, where residents \xe2\x80\x94 mostly poor people of color \xe2\x80\x94 have erupted\nin outbursts of anger and frustration because they feel they have little\nsay in the direction of their communities. Mr. Hogan could have supported\nthe law and reaffirmed that Maryland values the voices of all its citizens.\nInstead his veto means that 40,000 of them will have to wait until the\nLegislature reconvenes next January for a veto override vote.\n\n\n\nMr. Hogan\xe2\x80\x99s action is out of step not just with common sense, but with a\ngrowing segment of his own party. In recent years both red and blue states\n\xe2\x80\x94 from Texas to Connecticut to Alabama to New Mexico \xe2\x80\x94 have reduced or\neliminated barriers to voting for people coming out of prison. Maryland\nended its own lifetime ban on voting for those with felony convictions in\n2007.\n\n\n\nThis is a moment of great promise for new efforts to reform the nation\xe2\x80\x99s\ncriminal-justice system. Disenfranchising people who are already back in\ntheir communities is nothing but punitive and counterproductive, and\ndismantling such laws should be an easy call.\n\n\n\n*Congress Should Extend PATRIOT Act Provisions to Keep Us Safe\n<https://medium.com/@JebBush/congress-should-extend-patriot-act-provisions-to-keep-us-safe-e7c77224b3ef>\n//Medium // Jeb Bush \xe2\x80\x93 June 1, 2015*\n\n\n\nThe failure of President Obama and the U.S. Congress to preserve key\ncounterterrorism authorities before they expired is an abdication of their\nresponsibility to protect the United States.\n\n\n\nToday we have lost the metadata program, authorized by Section 215 of the\nUSA PATRIOT Act, an important tool in helping law enforcement and the\nIntelligence Community connect the dots between known foreign terrorists\nand potential operatives in the United States.\n\n\n\nCongress still has the opportunity to remedy this mistake and extend these\nimportant tools that have kept us safe. They should do so.\n\n\n\nIt is incredible that we would weaken our ability to protect the Homeland\nas ISIL, Al Shabbab, Al Qaeda, and other affiliated terrorist groups expand\ntheir reach.\n\nEven President Obama, who spoke often about protecting civil liberties,\nsupported these authorities as Senator and preserved the program when he\ncame into office. Backing down in the face of liberal interest group\npressure is just another failure of presidential leadership.\n\n\n\nIn 2013 new safeguards were put in place \xe2\x80\x94 including requiring the\ngovernment to seek an individual court order every time it queried the\ndatabase \xe2\x80\x94 but maintained the essential structure of a database of call\nrecords that can be searched in the event we have intelligence warnings of\nplots to the homeland.\n\n\n\nThat was the right call. Because in total, the PATRIOT Act has kept us\nsafe, plain and simple.\n\n\n\nDespite the fear-mongering of some, there is no evidence the bulk metadata\nprogram has ever been used to violate civil liberties.\n\n\n\nThe terrorist threat to the United States is growing. It is incredible that\nwe would weaken our ability to protect the homeland as ISIL, Al Shabbab, Al\nQaeda, and other affiliated terrorist groups expand their reach.\n'
